    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="e83fbadcd90941b28634eff59e125ec2">

        function getParameter(parameterName) {
            let result = null,
                tmp = [];
            location.search
                .substr(1)
                .split("&")
                .forEach(function (item) {
                    tmp = item.split("=");
                    if (tmp[0] === parameterName) result = tmp[1];
                });
            return result;
        }

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            queryParamsDelimiter: encodeURIComponent('?'),

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                let redirectURL = getParameter('redirectUri');
                if(redirectURL == null){
                    redirectURL = encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
                }
                if(redirectURL.indexOf('saml_referrer') < 0){
                    redirectURL += encodeURIComponent(((redirectURL.indexOf(this.queryParamsDelimiter) !== -1) ? '&' : '?') + 'saml_referrer');
                }
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + redirectURL;
            },
            retrieveEntity: function () {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // get the results
                            var results = res.data;
                            return resolve(results);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('retrieve entity error: ' + err);
                        });
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage duo to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {doi: doi, entityid: entityId, entityTitle: entityTitle, eventGroupKey: '59124c0b-1202-4f37-b1db-80e368d42bc5'}

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (let i in allSsoInstitutions) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();
                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err){
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

    </script>

        <div class="relative"><a href="#" data-db-target-for="0e406ce9-5c43-4e2d-b741-e0b43d1f1865" data-db-switch="icon-close_thin" aria-haspopup="true" aria-controls="0e406ce9-5c43-4e2d-b741-e0b43d1f1865_Pop" role="button" id="0e406ce9-5c43-4e2d-b741-e0b43d1f1865_Ctrl" aria-label="Tools" class="article-tools__ctrl"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Tools"></i><span>Tools</span></a><div data-db-target-of="0e406ce9-5c43-4e2d-b741-e0b43d1f1865" aria-labelledby="0e406ce9-5c43-4e2d-b741-e0b43d1f1865_Ctrl" id="0e406ce9-5c43-4e2d-b741-e0b43d1f1865_Pop" class="article-tools__block fixed dropBlock__holder"><ul role="menu" class="rlist w-slide--list"><li role="presentation" class="article-tool"><a href="https://onlinelibrary.wiley.com/action/rightsLink?doi=10.1002%2Fsstr.202300369&amp;mode=" role="menuitem" target="_blank"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-permissions-key"></i><span>Request permission</span></a></li><li role="presentation" class="article-tool"><a href="/action/showCitFormats?doi=10.1002%2Fsstr.202300369" role="menuitem" target="_self"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-export-icon"></i><span>Export citation</span></a></li><li role="presentation" class="article-tool"><a href="/personalize/addFavoritePublication?doi=10.1002%2Fsstr.202300369" role="menuitem" target="_self"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-favorites"></i><span>Add to favorites</span></a></li><li role="presentation" class="article-tool"><a href="/action/addCitationAlert?doi=10.1002%2Fsstr.202300369" role="menuitem" target="_self"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Track-citations"></i><span>Track citation</span></a></li></ul></div></div>

   <div class="abstract-group ">
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" lang="en" data-lang="en" lang-name="English" id="section-1-en" style="">
         <h2 id="d286915592" class="article-section__header section__title main abstractlang_en main">Abstract</h2>
         <div class="article-section__content en main">
            <p>Sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) have emerged as an attractive alternative for large-scale energy storage due to their low cost and high safety. The selection of cathode materials, which determine SIB performance, becomes a crucial aspect. Vanadium-based phosphates are known for their multielectron transfer properties and stable structure, but their intrinsic electronic conductivity is limited. In this regard, bimetallic substitution is an excellent modification method for improving the conductivity property. Herein, a novel Na<sub>4</sub>VMn<sub>0.7</sub>Ni<sub>0.3</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>@C (NVMNP@C) cathode with less vanadium is designed for SIBs, which exhibits outstanding electrochemical performance at 25 °C, delivering rate capacity of 67 mA h g<sup>−1</sup> at 3 A g<sup>−1</sup>, and long-term cyclability of remaining 74.6% after 4800 cycles at 2 A g<sup>−1</sup>. Furthermore, excellent low-temperature adaptability (82 mA h g<sup>−1</sup> at 20 mA g<sup>−1</sup>, 60 mA h g<sup>−1</sup> at 400 mA g<sup>−1</sup>, and 90.4% capacity retention after 230 cycles at 100 mA g<sup>−1</sup>) at −40 °C is also achieved. This unique work has broken a feasible pathway for high-performance SIB cathode at low temperature.</p>
            </div>
      </section>
   </div>
   <div class="pb-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="below-abstract-group"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div>
   
   <section class="article-section article-section__full">
      <section class="article-section__content" id="sstr202300369-sec-0001">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="sstr202300369-sec-0001-title">1 Introduction</h2>
         
         <p>The transformation of energy pursues more efficient and cost-effective energy storage technologies (ESTs). In addition to traditional lithium-ion batteries (LIBs) that occupied the portable devices and power vehicles market, some emerging ESTs such as lithium–sulfur (Li–S) batteries, sodium-ion batteries (SIBs), potassium-ion batteries (PIBs), and zinc-ion batteries (ZIBs), are also thriving.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#sstr202300369-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="sstr202300369-bib-0001_R_d286915584e452" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1 - J. Energy Chem. SusMat Adv. Mater. Chin. Chem. Lett. Chin. Chem. Lett.">1</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Among them, SIB arose as the attractive alternative for large-scale energy storage due to its low cost and high safety.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#sstr202300369-bib-0002" class="bibLink tab-link" id="sstr202300369-bib-0002_R_d286915584e457" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 2 - eScience J. Energy Chem.">2</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The selection of cathodic materials that determined SIB performance has become a crucial aspect.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#sstr202300369-bib-0003" class="bibLink tab-link" id="sstr202300369-bib-0003_R_d286915584e462" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3 - Science">3</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Vanadium-based electrode materials with high theoretical capacity are commonly applied in various types of secondary batteries (LIBs, SIBs, PIBs, ZIBs, etc.) as well as vanadium redox flow batteries, thanks to their multiple chemical valence states.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#sstr202300369-bib-0004" class="bibLink tab-link" id="sstr202300369-bib-0004_R_d286915584e468" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4 - Acc. Chem. Res. SmartMat Mater. Horiz.">4</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Vanadium-based polyanions are considered to be one of the future mainstream cathodes for SIBs because of their robust structural stability and favorable thermal stability,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#sstr202300369-bib-0005" class="bibLink tab-link" id="sstr202300369-bib-0005_R_d286915584e473" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5 - Nat. Commun.">5</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> and in which the Na super ionic conductor (NASICON) structured Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>, operating at 3.4 V, is widely used as the cathodic materials in SIBs.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#sstr202300369-bib-0006" class="bibLink tab-link" id="sstr202300369-bib-0006_R_d286915584e487" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6 - Small Struct. Adv. Mater.">6</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Nevertheless, the high cost of raw materials may cause hesitation before its actual application. As of now, the international price of vanadium raw materials is approximately $14 000 per ton, which undoubtedly accounts for a significant proportion of the manufacturing cost of secondary batteries using vanadium-based electrode materials.<span><sup><a href="#sstr202300369-bib-0004" class="bibLink tab-link" id="sstr202300369-bib-0004_R_d286915584e491" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4 - Acc. Chem. Res. SmartMat Mater. Horiz.">4</a></sup></span> In recent research, there has been a growing emphasis on identifying alternative active transition metals to replace vanadium, which aims to achieve similar effects while reducing cost pressure.</p>
         
         <p>The active transition metal elements mainly including cobalt (Co), manganese (Mn), nickel (Ni), iron (Fe), titanium (Ti), chromium (Cr), etc., are explored to substitute vanadium. Among them, Mn element that possessed affordable price (≈1/10 of vanadium) and security has attracted great attention, and thus the novel Mn-doped Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> and Na-rich Na<sub>4</sub>VMn(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> materials are developed, which exhibit the increased working plateau of 3.6 V generated from Mn<sup>2+</sup>/Mn<sup>3+</sup> redox couple, thereby raising the energy density.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#sstr202300369-bib-0007" class="bibLink tab-link" id="sstr202300369-bib-0007_R_d286915584e517" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7 - Nano Energy Adv. Energy Mater. Adv. Energy Mater.">7</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The Na<sub>4</sub>VMn(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> material thus demonstrates prospective applications in high-energy-density SIBs (theoretically around 390 Wh kg<sup>−1</sup>), and when pouch cells are assembled with hard carbon anode, the estimated energy density can reach ≈200 Wh kg<sup>−1</sup>. Nevertheless, the Jahn–Teller effect that arose from the presence of Mn<sup>3+</sup> can lead to severe structural collapse, resulting in capacity degradation of SIBs.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#sstr202300369-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="sstr202300369-bib-0008_R_d286915584e536" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - ACS Energy Lett. Energy Storage Mater.">8</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Therefore, the introduction of individual Mn has both its pros and cons, and further regulation is required to address this issue.</p>
         
         <p>To enhance the electrochemical performance of V–Mn polyanion materials, it is common to introduce additional heteroatoms through bimetallic doping, utilizing the synergistic effect of bimetallic substitution (i.e., promoting metal properties and conductivity as well as further stabilizing the structure).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#sstr202300369-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="sstr202300369-bib-0009_R_d286915584e544" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - Small Struct. J. Mater. Chem. A Nano Lett.">9</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> For instance, Lavela et al. demonstrated that replacing Mn with mass Cr could construct a durable and adaptable structure with fast Na<sup>+</sup> migration kinetics. The optimized Na<sub>3.2</sub>Mn<sub>0.2</sub>Cr<sub>0.8</sub>V(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>/C cathode showed a specific capacity of 65.3 mA h g<sup>−1</sup> at 5C and remained 67% of its capacity after 240 cycles at 1C.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#sstr202300369-bib-0010" class="bibLink tab-link" id="sstr202300369-bib-0010_R_d286915584e564" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10 - J. Power Sources">10</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Ghosh et al. investigated the Mg<sup>2+</sup> and Al<sup>3+</sup> substituted Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>VMn<sub>0.75</sub>M<sub>0.25</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> (M = Mg or Al) materials for SIBs. It was found that in addition to suppressing the Jahn–Teller effect of Mn<sup>3+</sup>, the generated Na<sup>+</sup> vacancies could enhance their Na<sup>+</sup> transfer kinetics and deliver over 80 mA h g<sup>−1</sup> at 5C as well as stably cycle for 100 times at 1C.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#sstr202300369-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="sstr202300369-bib-0011_R_d286915584e595" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - Small">11</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Besides, a novel cathodic material, Mn/Ti codoped Na<sub>3.7</sub>MnTi<sub>0.3</sub>V<sub>0.7</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>, was constructed by Lu et al. which delivered 53.2 mA h g<sup>−1</sup> at 10C.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#sstr202300369-bib-0012" class="bibLink tab-link" id="sstr202300369-bib-0012_R_d286915584e613" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12 - Mater. Lett.">12</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The bimetallic substitution strategy appears to be useful for the improvement of electrochemical performance, while its effectiveness in increasing rate capacity and cycle life is somewhat limited. In contrast, the current demand for energy storage applications is becoming more diverse, particularly the outstanding electrochemical performance at extremely low temperatures.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#sstr202300369-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="sstr202300369-bib-0013_R_d286915584e618" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - eScience Adv. Mater.">13</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Continuous exploration of suitable bimetallic substitution strategies is thus necessary for the development of V–Mn polyanion materials in SIBs with high rate (&gt;10C), long cycle life (&gt;1000 cycles), and low-temperature adaptability.</p>
         
         <p>Here, a less-vanadium Na<sub>4</sub>VMn<sub>0.7</sub>Ni<sub>0.3</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>@C (denoted as NVMNP@C) material has been fabricated via two-step solid-state method. Except for increased rate capability (67 mA h g<sup>−1</sup> at 3 A g<sup>−1</sup>) and extended cycle life (74.6% over 4800 cycles at 2 A g<sup>−1</sup>) at 25 °C, the NVMNP@C cathode also demonstrates boosted low-temperature kinetics by exhibiting high-discharge capacity (82 mA h g<sup>−1</sup> at 20 mA g<sup>−1</sup>), remarkable rate capability (60 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 400 mA g<sup>−1</sup>), and excellent cyclability (90.4% capacity retention after 230 cycles at 100 mA g<sup>−1</sup>) at −40 °C. Density functional theory (DFT) calculations unveiled that the importation of bimetals into the sodium vanadium phosphate can effectively reduce bandgap and bring stronger metal properties, accelerate Na<sup>+</sup>/e<sup>−</sup> transfer, and the formation of a robust structure. In summary, this unique work has broken a feasible path for high performance at SIB cathode low temperature.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="sstr202300369-sec-0002">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="sstr202300369-sec-0002-title">2 Result and Discussion</h2>
         
         <p>X-ray diffraction (XRD) was executed for the identification of crystallographic structure and phase composition of the synthetic vanadium-based polyanion materials. As shown in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#sstr202300369-fig-0001">1a</a>, the as-prepared Na<sub>4</sub>VMn<sub>0.7</sub>Ni<sub>0.3</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>@C can be indexed into trigonal phase with R-3c space group and the refined parameters are <i>a</i> = <i>b</i> = 8.92620 Å and <i>c</i> = 21.5337(1) Å. The refined XRD result of the as-synthesized Na<sub>4</sub>MnV(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>@C (denoted as NVMP@C) and Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>@C (denoted as NVP@C) is illustrated in Figure S1, Supporting Information. The NVMP@C exhibits high crystallinity without additional impurities and the parameters of <i>a</i> = <i>b</i> = 8.96431 Å, <i>c</i> = 21.4430(6) Å, and NVP@C correspond to the NASICON phase with the parameters of <i>a</i> = <i>b</i> = 8.71830 Å and <i>c</i> = 21.7790(9) Å. As indicated in Table S1, Supporting Information, the cell volume calculated using refined parameters demonstrates that bimetallic doping successfully enlarges the polyanion material from 1433.57 to 1485.83 Å<sup>3</sup>, thereby confirming the successful substitution of dual ions. Figure <a href="#sstr202300369-fig-0001">1b</a> shows the crystal structure model of Na<sub>4</sub>VMn<sub>0.7</sub>Ni<sub>0.3</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>. In this 3D open framework, the interconnected Na<sup>+</sup> channels are constructed by insular Ni/Mn/VO<sub>6</sub> octahedral sharing all the corners with PO<sub>4</sub> tetrahedra. X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS) was measured to investigate the valence states of contained elements in the as-prepared materials. The Mn 2<i>p</i> and Ni 2<i>p</i> peaks were detected in full XPS spectrum (Figure <a href="#sstr202300369-fig-0001">1c</a>), indicating that Mn and Ni ions are favorably introduced into the Na<sub>4</sub>VMn<sub>0.7</sub>Ni<sub>0.3</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>@C material. As illustrated in Figure <a href="#sstr202300369-fig-0001">1d</a>, the peaks at 516.79 and 523.6 eV are attributed to the V 2<i>p</i><sub>3/2</sub> and V 2<i>p</i><sub>1/2</sub> of V<sup>3+</sup>, respectively. In Mn 2<i>p</i> spectrum (Figure <a href="#sstr202300369-fig-0001">1e</a>), the peaks at 641.6 and 653.9 eV are pointed as the Mn 2<i>p</i><sub>3/2</sub> and Mn 2<i>p</i><sub>1/2</sub> of the Mn<sup>2+</sup> accompanied by the presence of a satellite peak (646.7 eV). Besides, the peaks at 873.8 and 856.1 eV are equivalent to Ni<sup>2+</sup> with the couple of satellite peaks (860.9 and 879.9 eV), indicating the successful incorporation of nickel (Figure <a href="#sstr202300369-fig-0001">1f</a>). The inductively coupled plasma (ICP) result illustrates that the content ratio of V:Mn:Ni in NVMNP is 1:0.7:0.3. The valence states of NMVP@C and NVP@C have been detected. All Na/Mn/V/P/O/C elements exist in the full XPS spectrum of NMVP@C (Figure S2a, Supporting Information), and Mn<sup>2+</sup>/Mn<sup>3+</sup> copresents in the Mn 2<i>p</i> narrow spectrum due to the surface oxidation (Figure S2b, Supporting Information), as well as V maintains tervalence (Figure S2c, Supporting Information). Na/V/P/O/C can be identified in Figure S3a, Supporting Information, and V<sup>3+</sup> lies in the NVP@C (Figure S3b, Supporting Information). In Figure S4, Supporting Information, the carbon content of NVMNP@C, NVMP@C, and NVP@C samples can be calculated to be 2.34%, 4.18%, and 3.47% through the thermogravimetric analysis (TGA), respectively.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="sstr202300369-fig-0001"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/b1b57358-89e0-44e8-8f1f-12e2683b5871/sstr202300369-fig-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/b1b57358-89e0-44e8-8f1f-12e2683b5871/sstr202300369-fig-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/53b88902-e08b-41ed-a5a5-83340575747e/sstr202300369-fig-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/b1b57358-89e0-44e8-8f1f-12e2683b5871/sstr202300369-fig-0001-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 1<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=sstr202300369-fig-0001&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fsstr.202300369" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) Rietveld refinement of the XRD pattern of the NVMNP@C material. b) Crystal structure of the as-constructed Na<sub>4</sub>VMn<sub>0.7</sub>Ni<sub>0.3</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>. c) Full XPS spectrum, as well as d) V 2<i>p</i>, e) Mn 2<i>p,</i> and f) Ni 2<i>p</i> spectra of NVMNP@C.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <p>A scanning electron microscope (SEM) was executed to observe the morphology of the vanadium-based polyanion. In <b>Figure</b> <a href="#sstr202300369-fig-0002">2a,b</a>, the overall NVMNP@C particles exhibit ellipsoidal shape with dimensions ranging from 100 to 300 nm, and the surficial-coated carbon layer which is derived from carbonization of sucrose and citric acid can be clearly observed. The SEM observation of NVMP@C (Figure S5, Supporting Information) shows similar morphology and particle size to NVMNP@C, suggesting that the doping of nickel has weak impression on the morphology of this material. However, the as-prepared NVP@C is a little different, displaying the overall appearance of aggregation and individual particle size of less than 100 nm (Figure S6, Supporting Information). A transmission electron microscope (TEM) was executed to deeply investigate the microstructure of NVMNP@C. As illustrated in Figure <a href="#sstr202300369-fig-0002">2c,d</a>, NVMNP@C particles (100–300 nm) are clearly observed, being consistent with the SEM results. Through the high-resolution TEM (HRTEM) in Figure <a href="#sstr202300369-fig-0002">2e,f</a>, the clear lattice space of 0.39 and 0.28 nm can be indexed to the (113) and (116) faces of the NVMNP@C, respectively, confirming the high crystallinity of the as-synthesized material. Besides, a thin carbon coating layer of ≈4 nm is detected (Figure <a href="#sstr202300369-fig-0002">2f</a>). Energy-dispersive X-ray spectroscopy result, as displayed in Figure S7, Supporting Information, shows a uniform distribution of Na, V, Mn, Ni, P, O, and C elements, confirming the successful dual ions substitution in the NVMNP@C cathode.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="sstr202300369-fig-0002"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/8259e4b8-9dbd-4ddd-ac17-ebfb9a25c842/sstr202300369-fig-0002-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/8259e4b8-9dbd-4ddd-ac17-ebfb9a25c842/sstr202300369-fig-0002-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/26557b82-86ec-414e-874b-c1682baae77c/sstr202300369-fig-0002-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/8259e4b8-9dbd-4ddd-ac17-ebfb9a25c842/sstr202300369-fig-0002-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 2<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=sstr202300369-fig-0002&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fsstr.202300369" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a,b) SEM, c,d) TEM, and e,f) HRTEM images of the NVMNP@C material.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <p>Assembled half-cells based on the as-prepared cathodes and Na metal anode were initially cycled at ambient temperature via the galvanostatic charge–discharge (GCD) technique. In the voltage range of 2.5–3.8 V, in addition to the reaction of V<sup>3+</sup>/V<sup>4+</sup> couple, there is also a redox reaction of Mn<sup>2+</sup>/Mn<sup>3+</sup> in the Na<sub>4</sub>VMn<sub>0.7</sub>Ni<sub>0.3</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>, which offers a portion of the capacity. Nevertheless, it is important to note that the activity of Ni is not stimulated within this voltage range, meaning that there is no capacity contributed by Ni redox. As a result, higher Ni/Mn ratios were not further investigated in this study (Figure S8, Supporting Information). <b>Figure</b> <a href="#sstr202300369-fig-0003">3a</a> shows the rate comparison of Na<sub>4</sub>VMn<sub><i>x</i></sub>Ni<sub>1−<i>x</i></sub>PO<sub>4</sub>@C (0.5 ≤ <i>x</i> ≤ 0.9) samples. It can be concluded that the as-prepared Na<sub>4</sub>VMn<sub>0.7</sub>Ni<sub>0.3</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>@C electrode material exhibits the best electrochemical performance (superior discharge capacity and rate performance). According to the comparison of charge–discharge profiles of all Na<sub>4</sub>VMn<sub><i>x</i></sub>Ni<sub>1−<i>x</i></sub>PO<sub>4</sub>@C (0.5 ≤ <i>x</i> ≤ 0.9) cathode materials, Na<sub>4</sub>VMn<sub>0.7</sub>Ni<sub>0.3</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>@C demonstrates the smallest polarization (Figure S9, Supporting Information). Additionally, the rate performance of three cathodes (Na<sub>4</sub>VMn<sub>0.7</sub>Ni<sub>0.3</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>@C, Na<sub>4</sub>VMn(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>@C, and Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>@C) were also examined (Figure <a href="#sstr202300369-fig-0003">3b</a>). At the moderate rate of 0.05 A g<sup>−1</sup>, the NVMNP@C cathode exhibits a discharge capacity of 107 mA h g<sup>−1</sup>, surpassing the NVMP@C of 98 mA h g<sup>−1</sup> and NVP@C of 78 mA h g<sup>−1</sup>. When the current density is increased, the NVMNP@C cathode can display discharge capacities of 97, 92, 86, 81, 78, and 71 mA h g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.1, 0.2, 0.5, 1, 1.5, and 2 A g<sup>−1</sup>, respectively. When cycled at 3 A g<sup>−1</sup>, this cathode can still maintain high capacity of 67 mA h g<sup>−1</sup>, illustrating the excellent high-rate capability of NVMNP@C cathode. GCD curves of the NVMNP@C are displayed in Figure <a href="#sstr202300369-fig-0003">3c</a>, and two discharge plateaus at 3.4/3.6 V can be observed clearly. The impedance and electronic conductivity were also tested for further verify the excellent electrochemical performance of Na<sub>4</sub>VMn<sub>0.7</sub>Ni<sub>0.3</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>@C. As illustrated in Figure S10 and Table S2, Supporting Information, Na<sub>4</sub>VMn<sub>0.7</sub>Ni<sub>0.3</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>@C shows the lowest impedance and resistivity, as well as the highest electrical conductivity among all the samples. Next, the cycle comparison between NVMNP@C, NVMP@C, and NVP@C cathodes was executed. The NVMNP@C demonstrates an initial capacity of 88 mA h g<sup>−1</sup> and remains 94.2% of its capacity over 200 cycles at 0.2 A g<sup>−1</sup>, which is superior to the NVMP@C (72.9%) and NVPC@ (85%). Furthermore, the NVMNP@C cathode exhibits an outstanding cyclability at 2 A g<sup>−1</sup> (Figure <a href="#sstr202300369-fig-0003">3e</a>), delivering initial 72 mA h g<sup>−1</sup> and remaining 74.6% capacity retention after 4800 cycles with ≈100% average Coulombic efficiency (CE), which is superior to the NMVP@C (&lt;60% after 1800 cycles) and NVP@C (70% after 1000 cycles). Noteworthy, the GCD curves of NVMNP@C cathode show similar profiles at different cycles (100<sup>th</sup>, 500<sup>th</sup>, 1000<sup>th</sup>, 2000<sup>th</sup>, 3000<sup>th</sup>, 4000<sup>th</sup>, and 4800<sup>th</sup>) at 2 A g<sup>−1</sup>, confirming the excellent reversibility (Figure S11, Supporting Information). Even at a higher mass loading (1 mg cm<sup>−2</sup>), the NVMNP@C also demonstrated great rate capacity and excellent cycling performance by remaining 70 mA h g<sup>−1</sup> over 500 cycles at high-current density of 1 A g<sup>−1</sup> (Figure S12, Supporting Information).</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="sstr202300369-fig-0003"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/de3a691d-b798-4077-8a7e-6514dea0d2bf/sstr202300369-fig-0003-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/de3a691d-b798-4077-8a7e-6514dea0d2bf/sstr202300369-fig-0003-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/daa762b2-bee9-4d50-a102-775f43e74882/sstr202300369-fig-0003-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/de3a691d-b798-4077-8a7e-6514dea0d2bf/sstr202300369-fig-0003-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 3<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=sstr202300369-fig-0003&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fsstr.202300369" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Rate capability of a) Na<sub>4</sub>VMn<sub><i>x</i></sub>Ni<sub>1−<i>x</i></sub>PO<sub>4</sub>@C (0.5 ≤ <i>x</i> ≤ 0.9) cathodes, as well as b) NVMNP@C, NVMP@C, and NVP@C cathodes. c) GCD curves of NVMNP@C cathode from 0.05 to 3 A g<sup>−1</sup>. d) Cycle performance at 0.2 A g<sup>−1</sup> and e) long-term cyclability of NVMNP@C, NVMP@C, and NVP@C cathodes at 2 A g<sup>−1</sup>.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <div class="paragraph-element">The influence of Mn/Ni cosubstitution on the electronic structure of NVP has a significant impact on its electrochemical performance. To verify this, first-principle calculations based on DFT were executed to investigate the electron density of states (DOS) of NVMNP, NVMP, and NVP. As DOS result illustrated in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#sstr202300369-fig-0004">4a</a>, NVP shows a bandgap of 1.70 eV, and orbital projected electron density of states (PDOS) demonstrates that V orbital possesses major contributions to total DOS. Through the DOS of NVMP in Figure <a href="#sstr202300369-fig-0004">4b</a>, conduction bands shift down forced by the introduction of Mn, and result in a significant diminishment of the bandgap to 0.87 eV. Finally, the PDOS of Ni orbital near the Fermi level further reduces the bandgap of NVMNP to 0.7 eV (Figure <a href="#sstr202300369-fig-0004">4c</a>). Overall, the continuous diminished bandgap demonstrates the electronic conductivity of NVP can be dramatically promoted by substituting dual ions (Mn and Ni) for V ions. The lowest bandgap of NVMNP@C cathode greatly confirms that bimetallic substitution can effectively strengthen the metal properties and electronic conductivity of vanadium-based materials, thereby enhancing its rate capability and cycle life in SIBs. Furthermore, cyclic voltammetry (CV) tests with scan rates (denoted by <i>v</i>) of 0.1, 0.2, 0.4, and 0.6 mV s<sup>−1</sup> were executed to analyze the Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion kinetics of all electrodes. In Figure <a href="#sstr202300369-fig-0004">4d–f</a>, A2 anodic peak and C2, C3 cathodic peaks for NVP@C electrode can be observed; additional paired peaks (A1, C1) at higher potentials for the NVMNP@C and NVMP@C electrodes can be defined. Figure <a href="#sstr202300369-fig-0004">4g–i</a> exhibits the fitting results of peak current (<i>i</i><sub>p</sub>) and square root of the <i>v</i> (<i>v</i><sup>1/2</sup>) for NVP@C, NVMP@C, NVMNP@C electrodes, the line relationship demonstrates the electrochemical process of all samples is diffusion-dominant. Through the fitting results, the apparent Na diffusion coefficient (<i>D</i><sub>app,Na</sub>) can be calculated by Randles–Sevcik<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#sstr202300369-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="sstr202300369-bib-0014_R_d286915584e1151" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - J. Power Sources">14</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> equation as below:

The <i>D</i><sub>app, Na</sub> of NVP@C is 7.69 × 10<sup>−11</sup> cm<sup>2</sup> s<sup>−1</sup> (A2 peak), 3.2 × 10<sup>−11</sup> cm<sup>2</sup> s<sup>−1</sup> (C2 peak), and 3.57 × 10<sup>−12</sup> cm<sup>2</sup> s<sup>−1</sup> (C3 peak). As for NVMP@C, the calculation results are 7.56 × 10<sup>−11</sup> cm<sup>2</sup> s<sup>−1</sup> (A2 peak), 3.38 × 10<sup>−12</sup> cm<sup>2</sup> s<sup>−1</sup> (A1 peak), 1.01 × 10<sup>−11</sup> cm<sup>2</sup> s<sup>−1</sup> (C1 peak), 1.18 × 10<sup>−11</sup> cm<sup>2</sup> s<sup>−1</sup> (C2 peak), and 5.63 × 10<sup>−12</sup> cm<sup>2</sup> s<sup>−1</sup> (C3 peak), respectively. Notably, by means of the synergistic effects of bimetallic substitution, the diffusion coefficient of NVMNP@C is greatly increased to 1.01 × 10<sup>−10</sup> cm<sup>2</sup> s<sup>−1</sup> (A2 peak), 1.4 × 10<sup>−11</sup> cm<sup>2</sup> s<sup>−1</sup> (A1 peak), 2.89 × 10<sup>−11</sup> cm<sup>2</sup> s<sup>−1</sup> (C1 peak), 6.07 × 10<sup>−11</sup> cm<sup>2</sup> s<sup>−1</sup> (C2 peak), and 8.12 × 10<sup>−12</sup> cm<sup>2</sup> s<sup>−1</sup> (C3 peak), respectively. The comparison of <i>D</i><sub>Na</sub><sup>+</sup> is summarized in Table S3, Supporting Information, which clearly demonstrates that the NVMNP@C cathode shows the higher Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion coefficient than that of NVP@C and NVMP@C, indicating the bimetallic substitution effectively enhances the Na<sup>+</sup> transport kinetics of the electrode.</div>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="sstr202300369-fig-0004"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/f89dc40b-9b7d-4386-93a2-b61843d7f340/sstr202300369-fig-0004-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/f89dc40b-9b7d-4386-93a2-b61843d7f340/sstr202300369-fig-0004-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/73e7ff24-380c-434d-893c-2cf746afc00d/sstr202300369-fig-0004-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/f89dc40b-9b7d-4386-93a2-b61843d7f340/sstr202300369-fig-0004-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 4<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=sstr202300369-fig-0004&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fsstr.202300369" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">The PDOS results of a) NVP, b) NVMP, and c) NVMNP. CV curves of d) NVP@C, e) NVMP@C, and f) NVMNP@C at different scan rates. The plots between the <i>i</i><sub>p</sub> and <i>v</i><sup>1/2</sup> for g) NVP@C, h) NVMP@C, and i) NVMNP@C.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <p>Ex situ XRD and XPS were measured to detect the structure/phase evolution of NVMNP@C electrode in the working window of 2.5–3.8 V. Ex situ XRD patterns from 10–60° and the enlarged patterns in 31–32.5° are illustrated in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#sstr202300369-fig-0005">5a,b</a>. In the charge process (from open circuit voltage (OCV) to full charge of 3.8 V), diffraction peaks of (104), (113), (221), (300), (314), and (226) are shifted to right, corresponding to Na extraction, and the phase at 3.8 V is consistent with the Na-deficient Na<sub>4−<i>x</i></sub>VMn<sub>0.7</sub>Ni<sub>0.3</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>@C. As discharged to 2.5 V, the migrated diffraction peaks fully return to the origin state of Na-rich NVMNP@C phase. Ex situ XPS was executed for validating the valence evolution of V and Mn elements at various charge/discharge states. In Figure <a href="#sstr202300369-fig-0005">5c,d</a>, the peaks of V 2<i>p</i><sub>3/2</sub> and Mn 2<i>p</i><sub>1/2</sub> in the origin NVMNP@C electrode are well indexed to V<sup>3+</sup> (517.0 eV) and Mn<sup>2+</sup> (653.9 eV). When fully charged to 3.8 V, the value of V 2<i>p</i><sub>3/2</sub> increases to 517.4 eV (V<sup>4+</sup>) and the Mn 2<i>p</i><sub>1/2</sub> increases to 654.5 eV (Mn<sup>3+</sup>), suggesting the working plateaus at 3.4 and 3.6 V are generated from V<sup>3+</sup>/V<sup>4+</sup> and Mn<sup>2+</sup>/Mn<sup>3+</sup>. When discharged to 2.5 V, the Mn 2<i>p</i><sub>1/2</sub> and V 2<i>p</i><sub>3/2</sub> shift back to the original state that is recurrence of Mn<sup>2+</sup> and V<sup>3+</sup>. Besides, the ex situ XPS spectra of Ni 2<i>p</i> at different states are illustrated in Figure S13, Supporting Information. The binding energies of Ni 2<i>p</i><sub>3/2</sub> and satellites remain almost unchanged when the cell is charged to 3.8 V and discharged to 2.5 V, further confirming that the nickel is not involved in the electrochemical process. Therefore, ex situ XRD and XPS results further confirm the reversible sodification/desodification mechanism of NVMNP@C.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="sstr202300369-fig-0005"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/92414637-c099-4f73-a2df-270263a6b179/sstr202300369-fig-0005-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/92414637-c099-4f73-a2df-270263a6b179/sstr202300369-fig-0005-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/72541442-c166-4a3a-b238-1526e03ee647/sstr202300369-fig-0005-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/92414637-c099-4f73-a2df-270263a6b179/sstr202300369-fig-0005-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 5<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=sstr202300369-fig-0005&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fsstr.202300369" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) Ex situ XRD patterns of the NVMNP@C cathode in 2.5–3.8 V and b) partial enlarged view. XPS spectra of c) Mn 2<i>p</i> and d) V 2<i>p</i> at various states for the NVMNP@C.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <p>On the basis of the above demonstrated advantages, the as-synthesized NVMNP@C cathodic material also displays the excellent low-temperature adaptability at −40 °C. Rate performance of NVMNP@C and NVMP@C samples are compared in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#sstr202300369-fig-0006">6a</a> (charge current density: 20 mA g<sup>−1</sup>). The NVMNP@C cathode can deliver high capacity of 82 mA h g<sup>−1</sup> at 20 mA g<sup>−1</sup> and possesses continuously stable discharge capacity from 20 mA g<sup>−1</sup> to 100 mA g<sup>−1</sup>. Furthermore, 60 mA h g<sup>−1</sup> at high discharge current density of 400 mA g<sup>−1</sup> can be achieved that is superior to NVMP@C cathode (40 mA h g<sup>−1</sup>), further verifying the outstanding rate capability of NVMNP@C cathode at low temperature. Besides, the NVMNP@C cathode also emerges high reversibility, by delivering 81 mA h g<sup>−1</sup> when it is back to 20 mA g<sup>−1</sup>. The CV measurement was also performed at −40 °C (Figure S14, Supporting Information), which further illustrates that the NVMNP@C equips with fast low-temperature reaction kinetics. Figure <a href="#sstr202300369-fig-0006">6b</a> displays the charge–discharge curves of the NVMNP@C from 20 to 400 mA g<sup>−1</sup>. As low as −40 °C, the NVMNP@C tenaciously remains low polarization (&lt;0.1 V) at 20 mA g<sup>−1</sup>; even fast discharged at 400 mA g<sup>−1</sup>, the electrode is also able to stay working plateau &gt;3 V and superior to NVMP@C cathode (Figure S15, Supporting Information). The service life and cycle stability of the NVMNP@C cathode at low-temperature have been greatly improved, which can be verified from cycle manifestation at different rates. When cycled at 20 mA g<sup>−1</sup>, the NVMNP@C cathode displays 82 mA h g<sup>−1</sup> and maintains 81 mA h g<sup>−1</sup> after 160 cycles, corresponding to high retention up to 98.2%. As a comparison, the NVMP@C shows lower capacity of 59 mA h g<sup>−1,</sup> remaining 91.5% after the same cycles (Figure <a href="#sstr202300369-fig-0006">6c</a>). When discharge rate further increased to 100 mA g<sup>−1</sup>, the NVMNP@C also exhibited superior cyclability of remaining 72 mA h g<sup>−1</sup> with 94.9% of its capacity after 200 cycles, surpassing the NVMP@C cathode (72.1%) in Figure S16, Supporting Information. Furthermore, even undergoing the fast charge/discharge process (100 mA g<sup>−1</sup>), the NVMNP@C can still deliver high initial capacity of 73 mA h g<sup>−1</sup> (CE: 90.5%) and remain 90.4% after 230 cycles, while the NVMP@C sample only delivers 51 mA h g<sup>−1</sup> and rapidly decays to 60% of its capacity after the same cycles (Figure <a href="#sstr202300369-fig-0006">6d</a>). GCD curves in Figure <a href="#sstr202300369-fig-0006">6e</a> show almost identical shape during the 1<sup>st</sup>, 10<sup>th</sup>, 50<sup>th</sup>, 100<sup>th</sup>, 200<sup>th</sup>, and 230<sup>th</sup> cycles, revealing the high reversibility of NVMNP@C at −40 °C. Besides, electrochemical impedance spectroscopy (EIS) at −40 °C was also tested to illustrate the excellent low-temperature adaptability of NVMNP@C cathode. As shown in Figure S17, Supporting Information, the <i>R</i><sub>ct</sub> value of NVMNP@C cathode (448 Ω) is much less than NVMP@C cathode (682 Ω), which indicates the lower charge transfer impedance and fast charge transfer of NVMNP@C.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="sstr202300369-fig-0006"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/9635bb48-ac95-4bc7-81a3-f1544b5549c9/sstr202300369-fig-0006-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/9635bb48-ac95-4bc7-81a3-f1544b5549c9/sstr202300369-fig-0006-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/e144d2b4-55ed-442f-b8e7-b1b3b1e34f92/sstr202300369-fig-0006-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/9635bb48-ac95-4bc7-81a3-f1544b5549c9/sstr202300369-fig-0006-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 6<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=sstr202300369-fig-0006&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fsstr.202300369" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) Rate comparison of NVMNP@C and NVMP@C cathodes at −40 °C. b) Charge–discharge curves of NVMNP@C cathode from 20 to 400 mA g<sup>−1</sup>. Cycle performance (−40 °C) of NVMNP@C and NVMP@C cathodes at c) 20 mA g<sup>−1</sup> and d) 100 mA g<sup>−1</sup>. e) GCD curves of NVMNP@C cathode at 100 mA g<sup>−1</sup> and −40 °C.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="sstr202300369-sec-0003">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="sstr202300369-sec-0003-title">3 Conclusion</h2>
         
         <p>In summary, bimetallic substitution strategy has been applied and greatly enhanced the rate capability, long-term cyclability, as well as low-temperature kinetic of the less vanadium polyanionic cathode in SIBs. At the ambient temperature, the NVMNP@C cathode can deliver an initial capacity of 107 mA h g<sup>−1</sup> at 50 mA g<sup>−1</sup>, shows high rate capacity of 67 mA h g<sup>−1</sup> at 3 A g<sup>−1</sup>, as well as maintains 74.6% of initial capacity after 4800 cycles at 2 A g<sup>−1</sup>. Furthermore, this cathodic material exhibits excellent low temperature (−40 °C) adaptability with high initial capacity of 82 mA h g<sup>−1</sup> at 20 mA g<sup>−1</sup>, excellent rate capacity of 60 mA h g<sup>−1</sup> at 400 mA g<sup>−1</sup>, and stable cyclability of remaining 90.4% capacity retention after 200 cycles at 100 mA g<sup>−1</sup>. Such superior electrochemical performance is primarily attributable to the synergistic effect (synergistic enhancement of metallicity and conductivity) brought by bimetallic substitution, greatly boosting the electronic conductivity and Na<sup>+</sup> transfer kinetics.</p>
         </section>
      <div class="article-section__content">
